Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
6 min
Abu Sahl al-Su'luki: A Polymath of Nishapur
Biography of Abu Sahl al-Su'luki, describing his birth, education, travels, and scholarly achievements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life, education, and vast learning of the Ash'ari scholar Abu Sahl al-Su'luki al-Naysaburi.

Core Idea
A single scholar mastered over a dozen sciences and studied with al-Ash'ari himself.

Imam Ibn 'Asakir presents the biography of a remarkable figure who combined jurisprudence, theology, poetry, and more. This passage traces his journey from birth in 276 AH to his studies in Iraq and his eventual return to Nishapur. You will see how his mastery of multiple disciplines earned him the title 'the ink of his time'.

Guided Path
1
Read the full biography
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get acquainted with his life story.
Notice the isnad that transmits this biography.
2
Note the chain of transmission
Identify the reporters: Ibn Furak, then al-Bayhaqi, al-Hakim, and the full isnad to Abu Sahl.
This shows the careful preservation of biographical reports.
3
Identify his areas of expertise
The text lists many sciences: fiqh, adab, linguistics, grammar, poetry, kalam, tafsir, ifta', Sufism, calligraphy, prosody. List them and reflect on his broad knowledge.
He is called 'the ink of his time' due to his vast learning.
4
Trace his travels and events
Note the key dates: born 276, first hadith 305, debates in 317, travel to Iraq 322, arrival in Nishapur 337. Reflect on the mourning assembly attended by scholars.
He secretly left Isfahan to return to Nishapur after his uncle's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو سهل الصعلوكي النيسابوري رحمه الله ذكر الأستاذ أبو بكر بن فورك ان أبا سهل رحل إلى العراق وقت الشيخ أبي الحسن ودرس عليه، كتب إلى الشيخ أبو نصر بن أبي القسم ابن هوازن قال انا أبو بكر أحمد بن الحسين البيهقى قال انا أبو عبد الله محمد ابن عبد الله الحافظ قال : محمد بن سليمان بن محمد بن سليمان بن هارون ابن عيسى بن إبراهيم بن بشير الحنفى العجلى الامام الهمام أبو سهل الصعلوكي الفقيه الأديب اللغوى النحوى الشاعر المتكلم المفسر المفتى الصوفى الكاتب العروضى حبر زمانه وبقية اقرانه رضى الله عنه ولد سنة ست وسبعين ومائتين وسمع أول ما سمع سنة خمس وثلاثمائة طلب الفقه وتبحر في العلوم قبل خروجه إلى العراق بسنين وانه ناظر في مجالس أبي الفضل البلعمى الوزير سنة سبع عشرة وثلاثمائة وكان يقدم في المجلس اذ ذاك ثم خرج إلى العراق سنة اثنتين وعشرين وثلاثمائة وهو اذ ذاك اوحد بين اصحابه ثم دخل البصرة ودرس بها سنين إلى ان استدعى إلى اصبهان وأقام بها سنين ونزلها فلما نعى إليه عمه أبو الطيب وعلم ان أهل اصبهان لا يتخلون عنه فى انصرافه خرج مختفيا منهم فورد نيسابور في رجب سنة سبع وثلاثين وثلاثمائة، وهو على الرجوع إلى الأهل والولد والمستقر من اصبهان فلما ورد جلس لمأتم عمه ثلاثة أيام فكان الشيخ أبو بكر بن اسحق يحضر كل يوم فيقعد معه هذا على قلة حركته وقعوده عن قضاء الحقوق وكذلك كل رئيس ومرؤوس وقاض ومفت من الفريقين فلما انقضت الأيام
And among them is Abu Sahl al-Su'luki al-Naysaburi, may God have mercy on him. The master Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that Abu Sahl traveled to Iraq at the time of the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) and studied with him. (He wrote) to the Shaykh Abu Nasr ibn Abi al-Qasim ibn Hawazin, who said: I was informed by Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Bayhaqi, who said: I was informed by Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Abd Allah al-Hafiz, who said: Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Harun ibn Isa ibn Ibrahim ibn Bashir al-Hanafi al-Ajali, the imam, the hero, Abu Sahl al-Su'luki, the jurist, the scholar, the linguist, the grammarian, the poet, the mutakallim, the exegete, the mufti, the Sufi, the scribe, the prosodist, the ink of his time and the remnant of his peers, may God be pleased with him. He was born in the year 276 AH and heard hadith for the first time in 305 AH. He studied fiqh and became deeply learned in the sciences years before his departure to Iraq. He debated in the assemblies of Abu al-Fadl al-Bal'ami the vizier in the year 317 AH and was presented in that assembly at that time. Then he left for Iraq in the year 322 AH, at which time he was unique among his companions. Then he entered Basra and taught there for years until he was invited to Isfahan and stayed there for years and settled there. When his uncle Abu al-Tayyib died and it was announced to him, and he knew that the people of Isfahan would not let him leave, he departed secretly from them and arrived in Nishapur in Rajab of the year 337 AH, intending to return to his family, children, and established home in Isfahan. When he arrived, he sat for the mourning of his uncle for three days. The Shaykh Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq would attend every day and sit with him, despite his limited movement and his abstention from fulfilling rights. Likewise, every chief and subordinate, judge and mufti from both groups (would attend). When the days passed...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Abu Sahl al-Su'luki al-Naysaburi. It begins with a report from Ibn Furak that Abu Sahl traveled to Iraq to study with Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Then a full chain of transmission (al-Bayhaqi, al-Hakim) provides extensive details about his lineage and accomplishments. He is described as a master of many fields: fiqh, adab, linguistics, grammar, poetry, kalam, tafsir, giving legal opinions, Sufism, calligraphy, and prosody. Born in 276 AH, he first heard hadith in 305 AH and mastered the sciences before traveling to Iraq. He debated before the vizier al-Bal'ami in 317 AH. He traveled to Iraq in 322 AH, then to Basra, then to Isfahan. After his uncle's death, he secretly left Isfahan for Nishapur in 337 AH, intending to return but stayed for mourning. The passage describes the respect shown by the scholars of Nishapur, including Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq, who attended his mourning assembly daily despite his own limitations. The text ends mid-sentence at 'when the days passed'.

Key Terms
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
النيسابوري
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو الفضل البلعمي
أبو بكر بن فورك
أبو بكر بن إسحاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might underestimate his breadth of knowledge, thinking he specialized in only one field.
Correction: The text lists over a dozen disciplines he mastered, including fiqh, kalam, tafsir, poetry, linguistics, and more.

Checkpoint
With which famous theologian did Abu Sahl al-Su'luki study according to Ibn Furak?
A) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari
B) Abu Mansur al-Maturidi
C) Ibn Surayj
D) al-Juwayni

Key Takeaway
Abu Sahl al-Su'luki was a polymath who mastered fiqh, kalam, tafsir, poetry, and more, studied with al-Ash'ari, and was deeply respected by the scholars of his time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Sahl mastered over a dozen sciences before traveling to seek knowledge from al-Ash'ari. When you look at your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge, what prevents you from delving into multiple disciplines? Are you satisfied with staying in one area? What one step will you take this week to expand your learning beyond your comfort zone?
?
He studied directly under the founder of the Ash'ari school. Do you have a personal teacher for your creed? If not, consider what that means for your connection to the transmitted tradition. How can you attach yourself to a living chain of knowledge that reaches back to the Salaf?
?
When his uncle died, great scholars like Abu Bakr ibn Ishaq attended his mourning daily despite their own limitations. How do you honor the scholars in your community? Do you show up for them in times of grief and need, or do you take their presence for granted? Write down one concrete way you will support a local scholar this week.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki: Teaching and Teachers
Ibn Asakir details Abu Sahl's teaching schedule and lists his teachers across three regions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly career of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, particularly his teaching schedule, his tenure in Nishapur, and the network of teachers he studied with across three regions.

Core Idea
A scholar's legacy is built on a solid foundation of teachers and consistent teaching.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari introduces us to the renowned Hanafi jurist and theologian Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. The biographer highlights the high regard he commanded, the formal teaching sessions he led, and the extensive list of scholars he learned from. Pay attention to the geographical breadth of his studies, which spanned Khurasan, Ray, and Iraq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on teaching schedule
Read the first part describing the daily sessions and his settlement. Notice the universal acknowledgment of his superiority and his 32-year leadership.
The phrase 'رأس اصحابه بنيسابور اثنتين وثلاثين سنة' is key.
2
Identify the three regions of his teachers
The passage lists his teachers in Khurasan, Ray, and Iraq. Count the names in each region.
There are seven names in Khurasan, two in Ray, and four in Iraq.
3
Reflect on his scholarly network
Consider how studying under multiple scholars across regions contributed to his authority. What does this say about the importance of isnad and travel in Islamic scholarship?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمعزي عقدوا له المجلس غداة كل يوم للتدريس والالقاء ومجلس النظر عشية الأربعاء واستقر به ولم يبق في البلد موافق ولا مخالف إلا وهو مقر له بالفضل والتقدم وحضره المشايخ مرة بعد أخرى يسألون ان ينقل من خلفهم وراءه بأصبهان فاجاب إلى ذلك ودرس وأفتى ورأس اصحابه بنيسابور اثنتين وثلاثين سنة . سمع بخراسان أبا بكر بن خزيمة وأبا العباس الثقفى وأبا على أحمد بن عمر بن يزيد المحمد أبا ذى وأبا العباس الأزهرى وأبا قريش الحافظ وأبا العباس الماسرجسى واقرانهم وسمع بالرى أبا محمد بن أبي حاتم وأبا عبدالله أحمد بن خالد بن الحروری واقـرانـهـمـا وسمع بالعراق أبا عبدالله المحاملي القاضى وأبا عبدالله محمد ابن مخلد الدوري. وإبراهيم بن عبدالصمد الهاشمي، وأبا بكر محمد بن القسم بن الانباري ،واقرانهم، ثم ان الأستاذ قعد للحديث عشية الجمعة وحدث الناس، قال أبو عبد الله سمعت أبا بكر أحمد بن اسحق الامام رحمه الله غير مرة وهو يعوذ الأستاذ أبا سهل وينفث على دعائه ويقول بارك الله فيك لا أصابك العين هذا في مجالس النظر عشية السبت للكلام وعشية الثلاثاء للفقه قال وسمعت أبا على الاسفراينى يقول سمعت أبا اسحق المروزى يقول ذهبت من مجلسنا بعد خروج أبي سهل النيسابوري وقال سمعت أبا الطاهر الأنماطى الفقيه بالرى يقول سمعت الصاحب أبا القسم يعني ابن عباد يقول لانرى مثله ولا رأى هو مثل نفسه يعنى أبا سهل وقال سمعت الفقيه يقول سئل أبو الوليد عن أبي بكر القفال وأبي سهل ايهما ارجح فقال ومن يقدر ان يكون مثل أبي سهل، قال أبو عبد الله سمعت أبا الفضل ابن يعقوب يقول سمعت أبا الحسن على بن أحمد البنوجردى يقول : كنت في حلقة أبى بكر الشافعي الصيرفي فسمعته يقول خرج أبو سهل الصعلوكى الى خراسان ولم ير أهل خراسان مثله، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم بن السمرقندى قال قال لنا الشيخ الامام أبو اسحق الشيرازي : أبو سهل محمد بن سلیمان بن محمد بن سلیمان بن هرون الصعلوكي الحنفي من بنى حنيفة صاحب أبى اسحق المروزي مات في آخر سنة تسع وستين وثلاثمائة وكان فقيها أديبا شاعرا متكلما صوفيا كاتبا وعنه اخذ ابنه أبو الطيب وفقهاء نيسابور سمعت أبا المظفر بن القشيري يقول سمعت أبي الأستاذ أبا القسم يقول سمعت أبا عبد الرحمن السلمي يقول : وهب
For al-Mu'azzi they held a session for him every morning for teaching and lecturing, and a session for debate on Wednesday evening, and he settled there, and there remained in the city no supporter nor opponent except that he acknowledged him to be superior and advanced. The shaykhs attended him time after time asking him to transmit from those behind him in Isfahan, so he agreed to that, taught, gave fatwas, and headed his companions in Nishapur for thirty-two years. He heard (hadith) in Khurasan from Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma, Abu al-Abbas al-Thaqafi, Abu Ali Ahmad ibn Umar ibn Yazid al-Muhammad Aba Dhi, Abu al-Abbas al-Azhari, Abu Quraysh al-Hafiz, Abu al-Abbas al-Masarsisi, and their peers. He heard in Ray from Abu Muhammad ibn Abi Hatim, Abu Abdullah Ahmad ibn Khalid ibn al-Haruri, and their two peers. He heard in Iraq from Abu Abdullah al-Mahamili the judge, Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Makhlad al-Duri, Ibrahim ibn Abd al-Samad al-Hashimi, and Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Qasim ibn al-Anbari, and their peers. Then the master (al-ustadh) sat for hadith on Friday evening and narrated to the people. Abu Abdullah said: I heard Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, more than once, and he was reciting ruqya over the master Abu Sahl and blowing on his supplication, saying: 'May Allah bless you, may the evil eye not affect you.' This was in the sessions of debate on Saturday evening for kalam and Tuesday evening for fiqh. He said: I heard Abu Ali al-Isfarayini say: I heard Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi say: 'He left our session after the departure of Abu Sahl al-Naysaburi.' And he said: I heard Abu al-Tahir al-Anmati the jurist in Ray say: I heard al-Sahib Abu al-Qasim, meaning Ibn Abbad, say: 'We have not seen his like, nor has he seen his own like,' meaning Abu Sahl. And he said: I heard the jurist say: Abu al-Walid was asked about Abu Bakr al-Qaffal and Abu Sahl: which of them is more preeminent? He said: 'And who can be like Abu Sahl?' Abu Abdullah said: I heard Abu al-Fadl ibn Ya'qub say: I heard Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Banujirdi say: I was in the circle of Abu Bakr al-Shafi'i al-Sayrafi and heard him say: 'Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki went out to Khurasan, and the people of Khurasan have not seen his like.' The shaykh Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi informed us, saying: The shaykh and imam Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi said to us: 'Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Harun al-Sa'luki al-Hanafi of Banu Hanifa, the companion of Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi, died at the end of the year 369 AH. He was a jurist, a man of letters, a poet, a theologian, a Sufi, and a scribe. His son Abu al-Tayyib and the jurists of Nishapur took knowledge from him.' I heard Abu al-Muzaffar ibn al-Qushayri say: I heard my father, the master Abu al-Qasim, say: I heard Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami say: 'Wahb...'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry about Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. It begins by describing his daily teaching and debate schedule, noting that he settled in a city and was universally acknowledged as superior. He taught for thirty-two years in Nishapur. The passage then lists his teachers across three regions: Khurasan, Ray, and Iraq, naming many prominent scholars. Following that, several testimonials from other scholars are given, affirming his unmatched excellence. One anecdote mentions that Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq used to perform ruqya for him, and another quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad saying he had not seen anyone like Abu Sahl. The biographical sketch concludes with his full name, tribal affiliation (Banu Hanifa), death date (369 AH), and his multiple scholarly virtues: jurist, man of letters, poet, theologian, Sufi, and scribe. It ends with an incomplete quote from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami starting 'Wahb...' which continues in the next anecdote.

Key Terms

نيسابور
المجالس
خراسان
الري
العراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many years did Abu Sahl head his companions in Nishapur?
A) 20 years
B) 32 years
C) 40 years
D) 25 years

Key Takeaway
Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki's scholarly authority was built on three decades of teaching and a diverse network of teachers across major Islamic centers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Sahl taught consistently for 32 years. What one area of knowledge have you committed to teaching or learning consistently, week after week, for even one year? If none, what is stopping you from starting today?
?
He learned from teachers across three regions. Who are the teachers who have shaped your understanding of Islam? Have you truly sat at their feet, or are you just a distant listener? What will you do this week to deepen your relationship with a living teacher?
?
The shaykhs asked him to transmit from those behind him. Do you have anyone you are actively mentoring or passing knowledge to? If not, why not? Write down one person you will share a lesson with this week.

Testimonials of Esteem for Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki
Ibn Asakir compiles testimonials praising Abu Sahl and concludes with his full name, death date, and virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the high esteem in which Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki was held by his contemporaries, as evidenced by multiple testimonials and his distinguished attributes.

Core Idea
A scholar's true measure is the praise of his peers.

In this continuation, Ibn Asakir presents a series of firsthand accounts and sayings that extol Abu Sahl's excellence. These range from a protective prayer recited over him to explicit comparisons that rank him above other notable scholars. The section concludes with a formal biographical note summarizing his full name, lineage, death date, and multiple fields of expertise.

Guided Path
1
Read the testimonials carefully
Read the Arabic passage of testimonials. Notice the chain of transmission for each report.
These are separate anecdotes, not a single narrative.
2
Identify the key themes of praise
Each testimony emphasizes his uniqueness and superiority. Compare the wording: 'لا نرى مثله', 'من يقدر ان يكون مثل أبي سهل', etc.
3
Examine the final biographical summary
Read Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi's description. Note the six attributes: faqih, adib, sha'ir, mutakallim, sufi, katib.
This summary encapsulates his scholarly identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمعزي عقدوا له المجلس غداة كل يوم للتدريس والالقاء ومجلس النظر عشية الأربعاء واستقر به ولم يبق في البلد موافق ولا مخالف إلا وهو مقر له بالفضل والتقدم وحضره المشايخ مرة بعد أخرى يسألون ان ينقل من خلفهم وراءه بأصبهان فاجاب إلى ذلك ودرس وأفتى ورأس اصحابه بنيسابور اثنتين وثلاثين سنة . سمع بخراسان أبا بكر بن خزيمة وأبا العباس الثقفى وأبا على أحمد بن عمر بن يزيد المحمد أبا ذى وأبا العباس الأزهرى وأبا قريش الحافظ وأبا العباس الماسرجسى واقرانهم وسمع بالرى أبا محمد بن أبي حاتم وأبا عبدالله أحمد بن خالد بن الحروری واقـرانـهـمـا وسمع بالعراق أبا عبدالله المحاملي القاضى وأبا عبدالله محمد ابن مخلد الدوري. وإبراهيم بن عبدالصمد الهاشمي، وأبا بكر محمد بن القسم بن الانباري ،واقرانهم، ثم ان الأستاذ قعد للحديث عشية الجمعة وحدث الناس، قال أبو عبد الله سمعت أبا بكر أحمد بن اسحق الامام رحمه الله غير مرة وهو يعوذ الأستاذ أبا سهل وينفث على دعائه ويقول بارك الله فيك لا أصابك العين هذا في مجالس النظر عشية السبت للكلام وعشية الثلاثاء للفقه قال وسمعت أبا على الاسفراينى يقول سمعت أبا اسحق المروزى يقول ذهبت من مجلسنا بعد خروج أبي سهل النيسابوري وقال سمعت أبا الطاهر الأنماطى الفقيه بالرى يقول سمعت الصاحب أبا القسم يعني ابن عباد يقول لانرى مثله ولا رأى هو مثل نفسه يعنى أبا سهل وقال سمعت الفقيه يقول سئل أبو الوليد عن أبي بكر القفال وأبي سهل ايهما ارجح فقال ومن يقدر ان يكون مثل أبي سهل، قال أبو عبد الله سمعت أبا الفضل ابن يعقوب يقول سمعت أبا الحسن على بن أحمد البنوجردى يقول : كنت في حلقة أبى بكر الشافعي الصيرفي فسمعته يقول خرج أبو سهل الصعلوكى الى خراسان ولم ير أهل خراسان مثله، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم بن السمرقندى قال قال لنا الشيخ الامام أبو اسحق الشيرازي : أبو سهل محمد بن سلیمان بن محمد بن سلیمان بن هرون الصعلوكي الحنفي من بنى حنيفة صاحب أبى اسحق المروزي مات في آخر سنة تسع وستين وثلاثمائة وكان فقيها أديبا شاعرا متكلما صوفيا كاتبا وعنه اخذ ابنه أبو الطيب وفقهاء نيسابور سمعت أبا المظفر بن القشيري يقول سمعت أبي الأستاذ أبا القسم يقول سمعت أبا عبد الرحمن السلمي يقول : وهب
For al-Mu'azzi they held a session for him every morning for teaching and lecturing, and a session for debate on Wednesday evening, and he settled there, and there remained in the city no supporter nor opponent except that he acknowledged him to be superior and advanced. The shaykhs attended him time after time asking him to transmit from those behind him in Isfahan, so he agreed to that, taught, gave fatwas, and headed his companions in Nishapur for thirty-two years. He heard (hadith) in Khurasan from Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma, Abu al-Abbas al-Thaqafi, Abu Ali Ahmad ibn Umar ibn Yazid al-Muhammad Aba Dhi, Abu al-Abbas al-Azhari, Abu Quraysh al-Hafiz, Abu al-Abbas al-Masarsisi, and their peers. He heard in Ray from Abu Muhammad ibn Abi Hatim, Abu Abdullah Ahmad ibn Khalid ibn al-Haruri, and their two peers. He heard in Iraq from Abu Abdullah al-Mahamili the judge, Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Makhlad al-Duri, Ibrahim ibn Abd al-Samad al-Hashimi, and Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Qasim ibn al-Anbari, and their peers. Then the master (al-ustadh) sat for hadith on Friday evening and narrated to the people. Abu Abdullah said: I heard Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq al-Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, more than once, and he was reciting ruqya over the master Abu Sahl and blowing on his supplication, saying: 'May Allah bless you, may the evil eye not affect you.' This was in the sessions of debate on Saturday evening for kalam and Tuesday evening for fiqh. He said: I heard Abu Ali al-Isfarayini say: I heard Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi say: 'He left our session after the departure of Abu Sahl al-Naysaburi.' And he said: I heard Abu al-Tahir al-Anmati the jurist in Ray say: I heard al-Sahib Abu al-Qasim, meaning Ibn Abbad, say: 'We have not seen his like, nor has he seen his own like,' meaning Abu Sahl. And he said: I heard the jurist say: Abu al-Walid was asked about Abu Bakr al-Qaffal and Abu Sahl: which of them is more preeminent? He said: 'And who can be like Abu Sahl?' Abu Abdullah said: I heard Abu al-Fadl ibn Ya'qub say: I heard Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Banujirdi say: I was in the circle of Abu Bakr al-Shafi'i al-Sayrafi and heard him say: 'Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki went out to Khurasan, and the people of Khurasan have not seen his like.' The shaykh Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi informed us, saying: The shaykh and imam Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi said to us: 'Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Harun al-Sa'luki al-Hanafi of Banu Hanifa, the companion of Abu Ishaq al-Marwazi, died at the end of the year 369 AH. He was a jurist, a man of letters, a poet, a theologian, a Sufi, and a scribe. His son Abu al-Tayyib and the jurists of Nishapur took knowledge from him.' I heard Abu al-Muzaffar ibn al-Qushayri say: I heard my father, the master Abu al-Qasim, say: I heard Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami say: 'Wahb...'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry about Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. It begins by describing his daily teaching and debate schedule, noting that he settled in a city and was universally acknowledged as superior. He taught for thirty-two years in Nishapur. The passage then lists his teachers across three regions: Khurasan, Ray, and Iraq, naming many prominent scholars. Following that, several testimonials from other scholars are given, affirming his unmatched excellence. One anecdote mentions that Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ishaq used to perform ruqya for him, and another quotes al-Sahib ibn Abbad saying he had not seen anyone like Abu Sahl. The biographical sketch concludes with his full name, tribal affiliation (Banu Hanifa), death date (369 AH), and his multiple scholarly virtues: jurist, man of letters, poet, theologian, Sufi, and scribe. It ends with an incomplete quote from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami starting 'Wahb...' which continues in the next anecdote.

الصعلوكي
الحنفي
أبو سهل
نيسابور
مجالس النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu Sahl was only a jurist (faqih).
Correction: The text explicitly states he was also a man of letters, poet, theologian, Sufi, and scribe.

Checkpoint
Which of the following was NOT one of Abu Sahl's attributes according to the final biography?
A) Jurist
B) Poet
C) Sufi
D) Merchant

Key Takeaway
Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki was unmatched in his time, excelling in fiqh, literature, poetry, theology, Sufism, and calligraphy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the great scholars said of Abu Sahl, 'We have not seen his like,' they were measuring character and knowledge, not popularity. In your own life, whose approval do you seek more: the scholars who will praise you for piety, or the people who applaud your worldly success? What would the scholars who know you best say about your devotion to truth?
?
The scholars performed ruqya for Abu Sahl to protect him from envy. Is there an area of your religious practice where you fear the evil eye of others? Have you sought protection through sincere reliance on Allah, or have you hidden your good deeds out of fear of jealousy? What one act of worship will you commit to keeping secret this week, purely for Allah?
?
Abu al-Walid could not even imagine anyone being equal to Abu Sahl. By this standard, how do you view your own contributions to the deen? Are you content with mediocrity, or are you striving to be one whose absence is felt when you leave a gathering? What concrete step can you take today to increase your knowledge and practice so that those around you miss your presence?

Abu Sahl's Poverty and Debate with the Delegation
Ibn Asakir narrates how Abu Sahl wore a woman's jubbah due to poverty and still outshone a delegation of scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ibn Asakir uses the anecdote of Abu Sahl to demonstrate that true knowledge and humility can overcome outward appearances and worldly status.

Core Idea
True knowledge shines brightest when stripped of worldly adornment.

This anecdote from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari illustrates the character of Imam Abu Sahl al-Isfara'ini. Despite extreme poverty and wearing a woman's jubbah, he debated and surpassed a delegation of scholars from many fields. The story teaches us that knowledge and humility, not outward appearance, determine a person's real worth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the original Arabic text and then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: جبة, دراعة, الوفد, صاحب الجيش.
2
Understand the context of poverty and the delegation
Notice how Abu Sahl's poverty is described: he had only a woman's jubbah to wear in winter. A delegation of top scholars from Fars arrives, which he must receive.
3
Examine the commander's reaction and Abu Sahl's response
The commander orders him to ride out, and Abu Sahl puts on a durra'a over the woman's jubbah. The commander complains that he is being made light of because of the jubbah.
4
Reflect on the outcome and the lesson
Abu Sahl then debates the entire delegation and his speech prevails in every discipline. Reflect on how knowledge and humility triumphed over outward appearances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهب الأستاذ أبو سهل جبته من انسان في الشتاء وكان يلبس جبة النساء حين يخرج إلى التدريس اذ لم يكن له جبة أخرى، فقدم الوفد المعروفون من فارس فيهم في كل نوع امام من الفقهاء والمتكلمين والنحويين فأرسل إليه صاحب الجيش أبو الحسن وأمره بأن يركب للاستقبال فلبس دراعة فوق تلك الجبة التي للنساء وركب فقال صاحب الجيش انه يستخف بی امام البلد يركب في جبة النسوان ثم إنه ناظرهم اجمعين وظهر كلامه على كلام جميعهم في كل فن
The master Abu Sahl was given a jubbah by someone in winter, and he used to wear a woman's jubbah when he went out to teach, because he had no other jubbah. Then the well-known delegation from Fars arrived, which included in every field an imam of the jurists, theologians, and grammarians. So the commander of the army, Abu al-Hasan, sent to him and ordered him to ride out to receive them. So he put on a durra'a over that woman's jubbah and rode. The commander said: 'He makes light of me – the leader of the city rides in a woman's jubbah!' Then he debated all of them, and his speech prevailed over the speech of all of them in every discipline.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote illustrating Abu Sahl's extreme poverty: he had only a woman's jubbah to wear. When a delegation of top scholars from Fars arrived, the commander ordered him to receive them. He wore a durra'a over the woman's jubbah. The commander was embarrassed and complained. However, when Abu Sahl debated the entire delegation, his knowledge dominated theirs in every field. The story highlights both his humility (he wore whatever he had) and his intellectual superiority (he defeated the delegation in all disciplines).

Key Terms
جبة
دراعة
الوفد
صاحب الجيش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The anecdote criticizes Abu Sahl's poverty.
Correction: Instead, it emphasizes his virtue and knowledge, showing that humility and true learning are more valuable than fine clothes.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Sahl wear over the woman's jubbah when he went to meet the delegation?
A) A turban
B) A durra'a
C) A cloak
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Abu Sahl's example shows that sincere knowledge and humility, not fine clothes, earn respect and God's blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This story shows that Abu Sahl wore a woman's jubbah because he had no other—yet he was not ashamed. What 'shame' or fear of others' opinions have you let hold you back from seeking or teaching knowledge sincerely? Will you repent from that and put your trust in Allah alone?
?
Abu Sahl debated experts in every field and his speech prevailed, all while wearing a borrowed and inappropriate garment. If you were stripped of all worldly status and appearance, would your knowledge and taqwa still stand? What are you actually investing in: your image or your soul?
?
The commander was embarrassed by Abu Sahl's attire, but Abu Sahl's knowledge silenced all criticism. Think of a time you felt embarrassed about something Allah provided (or didn't provide) for you. How can you let go of that and focus on what really matters—your relationship with Allah and benefiting others?

A Dream and a Poem: The Status of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki
Dream story about the Prophet visiting Abu Sahl, followed by a praise poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream vision and a praise poem both affirm the esteemed position of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki as a leading figure of the hadith school and reflect the Prophet's care for the hadith community.

Core Idea
A dream and a poem testify to Abu Sahl's leadership and the Prophet's assurance for his community.

Imam Ibn Asakir, in his defense of the Ash'ari school, often includes narratives that highlight the virtues of its early figures. This passage presents two such testimonials: a dream in which the Prophet himself visits the ailing Abu Sahl, and a poem extolling his excellence. Together, they reinforce the high regard in which Abu Sahl was held and the divine protection over the community of hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the first part of the passage, from 'The shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar' to 'of which I am the leader.' Notice the chain of transmission and the key detail: the scholar changed his madhhab after seeing the dream.
The dream shows the Prophet's concern for the hadith scholars, symbolized by visiting their leader.
2
Reflect on the Prophet's reassurance
When the scholar worries about disorder after Abu Sahl's death, the Prophet says: 'Allah does not let a band perish of which I am the leader.' Ask yourself: What does this imply about the hadith community?
The Prophet's statement emphasizes divine protection for the community that follows him.
3
Examine the poem's praise
Read the poem recited by Abu Mansur al-Qahistani. Identify the qualities attributed to Abu Sahl: generosity, debating skill, eloquence, high ambition, and widespread fame.
The poem uses vivid metaphors: stars for his qualities, jewels for his words, ambition above the Spica star.
4
Connect the two parts
Consider how both the dream and the poem serve to elevate Abu Sahl's status. The dream provides a divine endorsement, while the poem offers a human tribute. Together, they form a compelling portrait of a revered imam.

Source Text

أخبرني الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن الحسن البسطامي بقومس قال انا جدى أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد بسطام قال سمعت الشيخ أبا البركات ظفر ابن القاضي الامام نوح بن اسماعيل بن إبراهيم بن القسم بن الحكم القزويني قال سمعت أبا الحسن الايوبى المتكلم الواعظ رحمه الله قال كان أبو نصر الواعظ رحمه الله حنفي المذهب وكان في زمن الأستاذ الامام أبي سهل الصعلوكي رضى الله عنه انتقل من مذهب الرأى إلى مذهب اصحاب الحديث فسئل عن ذلك فقال رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في المنام مع أصحابه قاصدا لعيادة الأستاذ أبي سهل الصعلوكي وكان مريضا قال فتبعته ودخلت معه عليه وقعدت بين يدى النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم متفكرا قال فقلت ان هذا امام اصحاب الحديث وان مات أخشى ان يقع الخلل فيهم فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لى لا تفكر في ذلك ان الله تعالى لا يضيع عصابة انا سيدها، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو نصر بن القشيرى اجازة قال انا أبو بكر البيهقي قال انشدنا أبو عبد الله الحافظ قال انشدنا أبو منصور محمد بن إبراهيم النحوى القهستاني يمدح الأستاذ أبا سهل : عنه إمام الهدى اني لفعلك شاكر إمام الهدى انى بودك فاخر ابا سهل الحبر المقدم اصبحت لدى اياد منك غر ظواهر أأكفر إحسانا لبست جماله اذا لم تلدنى المحصنات الطواهر أبوسهل السباق في كل مجلس على الخصم سيف صارم الحد باتر له مكرمات يقصر الوصف دونها و من رام احصاء لها فهو قاصر فصال ابی سهل نجوم مضيئة وألفاظه المستعذبات جواهر و همته فوق السماك وذكره إلى كل اطراف البسيطة سائر
The shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Bistami in Qumis informed me, saying: My grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad Bistam said: I heard the shaykh Abu al-Barakat Zafar ibn the judge and imam Nuh ibn Isma'il ibn Ibrahim ibn al-Qasim ibn al-Hakam al-Qazwini say: I heard Abu al-Hasan al-Ayyubi, the theologian and preacher, may Allah have mercy on him, say: Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz, may Allah have mercy on him, was of the Hanafi school, and he was in the time of the master and imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, may Allah be pleased with him. He transferred from the school of opinion (madhab al-ra'y) to the school of hadith. He was asked about that, and he said: 'I saw the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in a dream with his companions, heading to visit the master Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki while he was ill. He said: So I followed him and entered with him upon him, and I sat before the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, reflecting. He said: Then I said: This man is the imam of the people of hadith, and if he dies, I fear that disorder will occur among them. The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said to me: Do not think about that, for Allah Almighty does not let a band perish of which I am the leader.' The shaykh Abu Nasr ibn al-Qushayri informed us by permission, saying: Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi informed us, saying: Abu Abdullah al-Hafiz recited to us, saying: Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Nahwi al-Qahistani recited to us, praising the master Abu Sahl: 'From him: O Imam of guidance, I am grateful for your deed. O Imam of guidance, I boast of your love. O Abu Sahl, the learned one, the foremost, you have become in my view manifest favors from you, brilliant. Shall I disbelieve in a benefaction I have worn its beauty, when the chaste and pure have not given me birth? O Abu Sahl, the forerunner in every assembly, against the opponent a sword, sharp-edged, cutting. He has noble deeds that description falls short of, and whoever attempts to enumerate them is deficient. The brilliant qualities of Abu Sahl are luminous stars, and his delicious words are jewels. His ambition is above the Spica, and his mention journeys to every edge of the earth.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: a dream story and a praise poem. The dream is narrated through a chain of transmitters. It tells of Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz, a Hanafi scholar who switched from the school of opinion (ra'y) to the school of hadith because he saw the Prophet visiting Abu Sahl in a dream. The Prophet assured him that the community of hadith would not be lost. This confirms Abu Sahl's high status as an imam of hadith. The poem, also with a chain, praises Abu Sahl for his generosity, his debating skill, his eloquence, his high ambition, and his widespread reputation. The poem uses metaphors: his qualities are stars, his words are jewels, his ambition is above the star Spica.

Key Terms
مذهب الرأى
مذهب اصحاب الحديث
المنام
الأستاذ
القصيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the dream proves Abu Sahl is infallible or that he is the only leader of hadith.
Correction: The dream shows the Prophet's care for the hadith community, not Abu Sahl's infallibility. The Prophet emphasizes the community's protection, not an individual's perfection.

Checkpoint

A) He read a book
B) He saw the Prophet in a dream visiting Abu Sahl
C) He debated Abu Sahl
D) He was ordered by the caliph

Key Takeaway
The dream and poem both affirm Abu Sahl's pivotal role and the Prophet's reassurance that the hadith community is divinely protected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson describes a scholar who changed his entire school of thought because of a dream. Have you ever received a clear sign from Allah that challenged a deeply held belief? If so, how did you respond? If not, are you open to such a transformation?
?
The Prophet assured that his community would not perish. Does this promise give you a sense of security, or do you still fear for the state of Muslims today? What steps can you take to truly trust in Allah's protection while still working for the good of the community?
?
Abu Sahl was praised for his eloquence and debate skill. Are you able to defend the truth with both knowledge and grace? What is one area of your faith where you feel inadequate in explanation, and what will you do to improve it?

Abu Sahl's Poverty and Debate Victory
Ibn Asakir narrates Abu Sahl's poverty and his triumph over a delegation from Persia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Abu Sahl's deep knowledge overcame the embarrassment of poverty, illustrating the Islamic principle that true honor lies in learning, not wealth.

Core Idea
True knowledge shines regardless of outward poverty.

This lesson presents a striking story from the life of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, a master of Ash'ari kalam and Shafi'i fiqh. Despite extreme poverty that forced him to wear a woman's cloak, he was called to debate a delegation of Persian scholars. The passage shows how his intellectual superiority silenced criticism and demonstrated the dignity of the seeker of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the specific details: the winter jubbah, the delegation, the commander's order, and the outcome.
Pay attention to the contrast between Abu Sahl's outward appearance and his scholarly ability.
2
Identify the conflict
Why did the commander complain? What did he say? (Ibn Asakir reports that the commander felt disrespected.) Understand the social context: a scholar appearing in women's clothing was a strong social shock.
The text says: 'He shows contempt for me; the imam of the city rides in a woman's jubbah.'
3
Reflect on the lesson
Despite the humiliation, Abu Sahl's knowledge prevailed in every art. What does this teach about the Islamic valuation of knowledge over material status?
This story is often cited to show that poverty does not disgrace a scholar; rather, ignorance is the true disgrace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأستاذ أبو سهل جبته من انسان فى الشتاء وكان يلبس جبة النساء حين يخرج إلى التدريس اذ لم يكن له جبة أخرى، فقدم الوفد المعروفون من فارس فيهم في كل نوع امام من الفقهاء والمتكلمين والنحويين فأرسل إليه صاحب الجيش أبو الحسن وأمره بأن يركب للاستقبال فلبس دراعة فوق تلك الجبة التي للنساء وركب فقال صاحب الجيش انه يستخف بی امام البلد يركب في جبة النسوان ثم إنه ناظرهم اجمعين وظهر كلامه على كلام جميعهم في كل فن
The master Abu Sahl had a jubbah from a person in winter, and he would wear a woman's jubbah when he went out to teach, because he had no other jubbah. Then the well-known delegation came from Persia, among them in every type an imam of the jurists, theologians, and grammarians. So the commander of the army, Abu al-Hasan, sent to him and ordered him to ride out to receive them. So he put a durra'a over that woman's jubbah and rode. The commander of the army said, 'He shows contempt for me; the imam of the city rides in a woman's jubbah.' Then he debated them all and his speech prevailed over the speech of all of them in every art.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote from the life of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, a prominent Shafi'i and Ash'ari scholar. It begins by describing his extreme poverty: he owned only a single jubbah (a cloak) given by someone, and in winter he had to wear a woman's jubbah when going out to teach because he had no other. When a high-profile delegation of scholars from Persia arrived, the local military commander ordered Abu Sahl to receive them. To avoid embarrassment, he wore a durra'a (a type of outer garment) over his woman's jubbah. The commander was offended, thinking Abu Sahl was mocking him by wearing women's clothing. However, when the debates began, Abu Sahl's knowledge overwhelmed all the visiting experts in every field, proving that his worth was not in his attire but in his learning.

Key Terms
جبة
دراعة
وفد
ناظرهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Abu Sahl's choice of clothing was intended to show arrogance or disrespect.
Correction: The text clarifies that he had no other jubbah; his poverty forced him to wear a woman's garment. The commander's complaint stemmed from social norms, not from Abu Sahl's intention.

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Sahl wear a woman's jubbah when teaching?
A) He wanted to show humility.
B) He had no other jubbah due to poverty.
C) It was the custom in Nishapur.
D) He was imitating the women of his household.

Key Takeaway
The story of Abu Sahl shows that outward poverty does not diminish the honor of a true scholar; knowledge is the true source of dignity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a teacher or scholar who appears poor or shabbily dressed, does that affect your respect for them? How would you feel if you were in Abu Sahl's position, wearing a woman's jubbah because you had no other? Would you still have the courage to represent your community, or would you let pride prevent you?
?
Do you ever let your own lack of material things—like money, clothes, or status—stop you from pursuing or sharing knowledge? Abu Sahl wore a woman's jubbah to teach. What excuses do you make that hold you back from seeking or spreading knowledge?
?
Think of a time you felt ashamed of your appearance. Did that shame prevent you from doing something important? How can this story change your perspective on the true source of dignity and honor?

Dream Leads to Madhhab Change
Ibn Asakir relates Abu Nasr's dream of the Prophet, leading him to abandon the Hanafi school for hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream of the Prophet influenced Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz to adopt the school of hadith and the significance of such dreams in Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
A prophetic dream can redirect a scholar's entire madhhab.

This lesson presents a remarkable biography of a Hanafi scholar who, after a dream of the Prophet, transferred to the school of hadith. The story highlights the reverence for Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki and the Prophet's personal assurance for his community. Through this narrative, we see how personal spiritual experiences can affirm theological commitments.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the chain of transmission (isnad) that authenticates the story.
The isnad shows the care in preserving this account.
2
Identify the characters
Identify Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz, the dreamer, and Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, the ill scholar. Understand their roles and madhhabs.
3
Understand the dream's message
Abu Nasr saw the Prophet visiting the ill imam and was worried. The Prophet's response gave him confidence in the hadith school's endurance.
The dream directly caused his madhhab change.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this dream served as a personal validation for Abu Nasr and what it teaches about the respect for hadith scholarship.
This is not a doctrinal proof but a personal experience.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرني الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن الحسن البسطامي بقومس قال انا جدى أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد بسطام قال سمعت الشيخ أبا البركات ظفر ابن القاضي الامام نوح بن اسماعيل بن إبراهيم بن القسم بن الحكم القزويني قال سمعت أبا الحسن الايوبى المتكلم الواعظ رحمه الله قال كان أبو نصر الواعظ رحمه الله حنیفی المذهب وكان في زمن الأستاذ الامام أبي سهل الصعلوكي رضى الله عنه انتقل من مذهب الرأى إلى مذهب اصحاب الحديث فسئل عن ذلك فقال رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في المنام مع أصحابه قاصدا لعيادة الأستاذ أبي سهل الصعلوكي وكان مريضا قال فتبعته ودخلت معه عليه وقعدت بين يدى النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم متفكرا قال فقلت ان هذا امام اصحاب الحديث وان مات أخشى ان يقع الخلل فيهم فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لى لا تفكر في ذلك ان الله تعالى لا يضيع عصابة انا سيدها
I was informed by the shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Bistami in Qumis, who said: My grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad Bistam told us, who said: I heard the shaykh Abu al-Barakat Zafar ibn al-Qadi al-Imam Nuh ibn Ismail ibn Ibrahim ibn al-Qasim ibn al-Hakam al-Qazwini, who said: I heard Abu al-Hasan al-Ayyubi, the theologian and preacher, may Allah have mercy on him, say: Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz, may Allah have mercy on him, was of the Hanafi school of law, and in the time of the master imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, may Allah be pleased with him, he transferred from the school of opinion to the school of hadith scholars. He was asked about that and said: I saw the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in a dream along with his companions, heading to visit the master Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, and he was ill. He said: I followed him and entered with him upon him, and I sat before the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, reflecting. He said: I said, 'This is the imam of the hadith scholars; if he dies, I fear that disorder will occur among them.' The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said to me: 'Do not worry about that. Indeed, Allah Almighty does not let a group perish of whom I am the master.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a dream narrative reported through a chain of transmission. It recounts how Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz, originally a Hanafi (follower of the school of opinion), converted to the school of hadith (Ahl al-Hadith) during the lifetime of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. When asked why, he explained his dream: he saw the Prophet Muhammad and his companions visiting the ill Abu Sahl. Abu Nasr followed them and sat before the Prophet, worried that if Abu Sahl died, the hadith school would fall into disarray. The Prophet reassured him that Allah would not cause a community of which he is the master to perish. This dream signifies the Prophet's special care for the hadith scholars and the high status of Abu Sahl. It also illustrates how dreams were considered a legitimate reason for changing one's madhhab.

Key Terms
حنیفی المذهب
مذهب الرأى
مذهب اصحاب الحديث
رأيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في المنام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dream proves that the Hanafi school is false or inferior.
Correction: The dream is a personal experience that affirmed Abu Nasr's confidence in the hadith school; it does not invalidate other madhhabs.

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Nasr al-Wa'iz change from the Hanafi school to the hadith school?
A) He was convinced by Abu Sahl's arguments.
B) He had a dream in which the Prophet praised the hadith school.
C) He saw the Prophet visiting Abu Sahl and was reassured by the Prophet's words.
D) He wanted to gain favor with the ruler.

Key Takeaway
A dream of the Prophet can inspire a scholar to change his madhhab, but such personal experiences do not constitute a general proof against other schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between a school of thought you follow and an experience that seems to guide you elsewhere, do you have the courage to change, even if others may question your motives? What would it take for you to have that certainty?
?
Abu Nasr's fear was for the community of hadith scholars. In your own circle, what group of believers do you worry about most? What are you doing to protect their unity and faith?
?
This dream gave Abu Nasr a direct message from the Prophet. If you were granted a similar dream but it contradicted a strongly held belief, would you accept it? Why or why not? What safeguards do you have against being misled by personal experiences?

Poem in Praise of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki
Abu Mansur al-Nahwi al-Qahistani recites a poem praising Imam Abu Sahl's virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the qualities attributed to Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki as expressed through a panegyric poem by Abu Mansur al-Nahwi al-Qahistani.

Core Idea
Abu Sahl's knowledge revived the Sharia, and his virtues are unmatched.

This lesson presents a poem composed in honor of Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, a prominent scholar of Khorasan. The poet, Abu Mansur al-Nahwi al-Qahistani, uses vivid imagery to praise Abu Sahl's intellect, debating skill, and role in preserving the Sharia. As you read the poem, consider how the hyperbolic language reflects the high esteem scholars held for one another.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once, then read the literal translation. Let the rhythm and imagery come through before analyzing.
2
Identify key praises
Notice the specific attributes the poet emphasizes: Imam of guidance, forerunner in debate, reviver of Sharia, unmatched excellence.
Look for lines like 'إمام الهدى', 'أبوسهل السباق', 'أحيا الشريعة'.
3
Examine the rival's portrayal
The poet contrasts Abu Sahl with his rivals, who are depicted as dull and ineffectual. Consider why this contrast is effective in panegyric.
4
Reflect on the poem's purpose
Think about why such praise poems were composed in scholarly circles. What do they tell us about the values of the community?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو نصر بن القشيرى اجازة قال انا أبو بكر البيهقي قال انشدنا أبو عبد الله الحافظ قال انشدنا أبو منصور محمد بن إبراهيم النحوى القهستاني يمدح الأستاذ أبا سهل : إمام الهدى اني لفعلك شاكر إمام الهدى انى بودك فاخر ابا سهل الحبر المقدم اصبحت لدى اياد منك غر ظواهر أأكفر إحسانا لبست جماله اذا لم تلدنى المحصنات الطواهر أبوسهل السباق في كل مجلس على الخصم سيف صارم الحد باتر له مكرمات يقصر الوصف دونها و من رام احصاء لها فهو قاصر فصال ابی سهل نجوم مضيئة وألفاظه المستعذبات جواهر و همته فوق السماك وذكره إلى كل اطراف البسيطة سائر أحار أبا سهل وفيك تحيرى وما انا في مستعجم الأمر حائر فيا عجبا من واحد سبق الورى فمافيهم مثل له ومفاخر لعمرى لقد أحيا الشريعة علمه ولولاه اضحى رسمها وهو دائر مساميه يبغى ابعد الشأو في العلا وهل مدرك شأو المها (قط حافر ألا اقصروا أنى لكم مثل فهمه وذلك بحر موجه الدهر زاخر هم يسهرون الليل في ضبط حجة تزول اذا ماجاش للشيخ خاطر هو الصدر والمتبوع في كل مجلس وعن رأيه العالي مـبـاهـيـه صـادر اغار عليه حين ينثر دره اذا وطيء المنشور من ذاك باقر ويوحشني مهما يساميه مفحم كليل بطيء بالسفاهة خابر ودادى له هز القريض وصاغه ومالي من طبع ومـا انـا شـاعـر بلوت فما فيهم سواك مظاهر فأنت امام الدین عندى ظاهر بقبت وسهلا ما اقام متالع وما ناح قـمـرى وغرد طائر
We were informed by Shaykh Abu Nasr ibn al-Qushayri by permission, he said: Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi told us, he said: Abu Abdullah al-Hafiz recited to us, he said: Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Nahwi al-Qahistani recited to us, praising the master Abu Sahl: Imam of guidance, I am grateful for your deed; Imam of guidance, I boast of my love for you. O Abu Sahl, the distinguished scholar, your favors upon me are manifest bright. Can I deny the goodness whose beauty I wore, if the pure protected women did not give birth to me? Abu Sahl, the forerunner in every gathering, against the opponent a sharp-edged sword, a cutter. He has honors that description falls short of, and whoever tries to enumerate them is incapable. The addresses of Abu Sahl are shining stars, and his sweet words are jewels. His ambition is above the Spica star, and his fame travels to all parts of the earth. O answerer, Abu Sahl, I am perplexed in you, and I am not in an ambiguous matter perplexed. So, what a wonder is one who surpassed mankind, for among them there is no like of him and no boastful. By my life, his knowledge revived the Sharia; were it not for him, its trace would have become erased and vanished. His rival seeks the farthest reach in highness, but can a hoof ever reach the pace of the wild cow? No, you are too far; how can you have the like of his understanding? That is a sea, its waves abundant for ages. They stay awake at night memorizing an argument that disappears when the shaykh's mind surges. He is the chief and the followed in every gathering, and from his lofty opinion, his rivals issue forth. They envy him when he scatters his pearls; when the spread is stepped on, that one is a gap-toothed beast. And it scares me whenever one equals him, a dullard, slow, acquainted with foolishness. My affection prompts him to shake poetry and fashion it, but I have no nature and I am not a poet. I have tested, and among them there is no manifestation like you; you are the manifest imam of the religion in my view. By your grave and Sahl, as long as the wild cow stands, and a turtledove coos and a bird warbles.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a poem of praise (madih) for Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, transmitted through a chain including Abu Nasr ibn al-Qushayri and al-Bayhaqi. The poet, Abu Mansur al-Nahwi al-Qahistani, lauds Abu Sahl's virtues: he is the imam of guidance, a forerunner in debates, sharp as a sword against opponents. His words are jewels, his ambition surpasses the stars, and his fame spreads worldwide. The poet expresses bewilderment at Abu Sahl's uniqueness, stating no one equals him. He credits Abu Sahl's knowledge with reviving the Sharia, without which it would have perished. The poet contrasts rivals who struggle in vain to match him, staying awake memorizing arguments that vanish before Abu Sahl's insight. He concludes by affirming that Abu Sahl is the manifest imam of the religion, swearing by his grave and by the natural world. The poem reflects the high esteem in which Abu Sahl was held by his contemporaries in Khorasan.

Key Terms
إمام الهدى
الحبر المقدم
السماك
الشريعة
مفاخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem's hyperbolic praise should be understood as literal doctrinal claims of infallibility or superiority beyond human measure.
Correction: The poem is a conventional panegyric, typical of the genre, and its exaggerated language is meant to honor the scholar, not to assert theological uniqueness.

Checkpoint
What does the poet claim would have happened to the Sharia without Abu Sahl?
A) It would have been corrupted by innovation.
B) Its trace would have been erased and vanished.
C) It would have been replaced by philosophy.
D) It would have become the dominant law.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Sahl was celebrated as a reviver of the Sharia and an unmatched scholar in his time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poet says Abu Sahl's knowledge revived the Sharia. In your own life, has your religious knowledge grown or weakened this year? What will you do today to begin reviving a neglected area of your deen?
?
Imam Abu Sahl was described as unmatched, a forerunner in every gathering. Do you settle for being average in your Islamic learning, or do you strive for mastery? Name one discipline you will commit to excelling in this month.
?
The poet admits he is perplexed by Abu Sahl's greatness and even scared of rivals? Do you feel threatened when you see others more knowledgeable than you? How can you turn that into motivation for your own growth instead of envy?

The Dream of Abu Sahl: The Power of Good Opinion of Allah
Ibn Asakir reports a dream where Abu Sahl explains his high station as due to good opinion of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of having a good opinion of Allah (husn al-zann) as exemplified in a dream of a great scholar.

Core Idea
Good opinion of Allah is the key to spiritual beauty.

This lesson presents a brief but profound dream narrative from the biography of a great Shafi'i scholar. In the dream, a student sees his deceased teacher in a state of indescribable beauty and asks how he attained it. The answer reveals a foundational attitude in Islam: having a good opinion of Allah. Let's examine this report and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream report
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the story. Notice the chain of transmission and the description of the dream.
Pay attention to the repetition at the end.
2
Understand 'husn al-zann billah'
Reflect on what 'good opinion of Allah' means in Islamic spirituality. It is a deep trust in Allah's mercy based on His promises, not naive optimism.
The repetition emphasizes its importance.
3
Apply the lesson
Consider how this attitude influences one's relationship with Allah and one's life. Ask yourself: How can I cultivate good opinion of Allah in my daily life?
Remember that this comes after a life of piety, not as a substitute for action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر بن الأستاذ أبى القسم قال انا أبي قال سمعت أبا بكر بن إشكاب يقول رأيت الأستاذ أبا سهل الصعلوكي في المنام على هيئة حسنة لاتوصف، فقلت له يا أستاذ بماذا نلت هذا؟ فقال بحسن ظنی برربی بحسن ظنی برربی
We were informed by Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar ibn al-Ustadh Abi al-Qasim, who said: My father told us, who said: I heard Abu Bakr ibn Ishkab say: I saw the master Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki in a dream in a beautiful state that defies description. I said to him: 'O master, what did you attain this with?' He said: 'By my good opinion of my Lord, by my good opinion of my Lord.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a short dream narrative reported by Abu Bakr ibn Ishkab, who saw Imam Abu Sahl in a dream after his death, in an indescribably beautiful state. When asked how he achieved such a high station, Abu Sahl replied that it was due to his good opinion of his Lord (husn al-zann billah). The repetition emphasizes the importance of this attitude. This teaches that having a positive and trusting view of Allah's mercy and promises is a key factor in attaining spiritual excellence. It aligns with the Sunni emphasis on hope in Allah's mercy alongside fear.

Key Terms
حسن الظن بالله
هيئة حسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That simply having a good opinion of Allah without any deeds ensures salvation.
Correction: The dream shows Abu Sahl after a life of scholarship and piety; his good opinion was the culmination of his devotion, not a replacement for action.

Checkpoint
According to the dream, what did Abu Sahl attribute his beautiful state to?
A) His extensive knowledge.
B) His good opinion of his Lord.
C) His chains of transmission.
D) His debates with opponents.

Key Takeaway
Having a good opinion of Allah is a powerful spiritual station that can be attained through a life of sincere devotion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your state after death, do you truly have a good opinion of Allah's mercy, or do you secretly doubt that He will forgive your shortcomings? What habit can you start today to strengthen that hope?
?
Abu Sahl's beautiful state in the dream was attributed solely to his good opinion of his Lord. Is your good opinion of Allah based on your own deserving or on His boundless generosity? How might you shift your perspective to rely more on His mercy than on your own deeds?
?
If you were to be asked in your grave, 'What did you attain this with?', what would your answer be? What would you need to change in your heart to be able to say what Abu Sahl said?

Biography of Abu Zayd al-Marwazi
Ibn Asakir presents a biographical entry on the Shafi'i scholar Abu Zayd al-Marwazi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly stature, travels, and narrations of Abu Zayd al-Marwazi as recorded by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Abu Zayd al-Marwazi: a top Shafi'i scholar, ascetic, and transmitter of Sahih al-Bukhari from al-Firabri.

This lesson presents a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari about the renowned Shafi'i scholar Abu Zayd al-Marwazi. Through this passage, we learn about his Ash'ari affiliation, his exceptional memory and asceticism, and his travels for knowledge and Hajj. It also highlights his role in transmitting Sahih al-Bukhari via the esteemed chain of al-Firabri.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical entry
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Abu Zayd al-Marwazi's life and achievements.
2
Identify key details
Note the attributes listed: his memory of the Shafi'i school, his reasoning skills, his asceticism.
These qualities mark him as a leading imam.
3
Trace the travel narrative
Follow his journeys to Nishapur, his stay in Mecca, and his narrations in Mecca and Baghdad.
Pay attention to the dates and purposes of each visit.
4
Consider the isnad
Notice the chain of transmission from Ibn Asakir back to Abu Zayd al-Marwazi.
This demonstrates the scholarly lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو زيد المروزى رحمه الله ) ذكر أبو بكر بن فورك انه ممن استفاد من أبى الحسن الأشعرى من أهل خراسان قرأت على أبى القسم زاهر بن طاهر المعدل عن أبي بكر أحمد بن الحسين الحافظ قال انا أبو عبد الله محمد بن عبد الله الحافظ قال : محمد بن أحمد بن عبد الله الفقيه الزاهد أبو زيد المروزي ، وكان احد أئمة المسلمين احفظ الناس لمذهب الشافعي وأحسنهم نظرا وأزهدهم في الدنيا قدم نیسابور غير مرة أولها للتفقه قبل الخروج إلى العراق وبعده المتوجهه إلى غزو الروم وقدمها الكرة الخامسة متوجها إلى الحج في شعبان سنة خمس وخمسين وثلاثمائة وأقام بمكة سبع سنين وحدث بمكة وببغداد بالجامع لمحمد بن اسماعيل عن الفربرى، وهى اجل الروايات لجلالة أبي من الصحيح
And among them is Abu Zayd al-Marwazi, may Allah have mercy on him. Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that he was among those who benefited from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari from the people of Khorasan. I read upon Abu al-Qasim Zahir ibn Tahir al-Mu'addil from Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Hafiz, who said: Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Hafiz told us, who said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Abdullah, the jurist and ascetic, Abu Zayd al-Marwazi. He was one of the imams of the Muslims, the most retentive of people of the Shafi'i school, the best in reasoning, and the most ascetic in this world. He came to Nishapur multiple times: first for fiqh study before leaving for Iraq, and after that heading to the Byzantine campaign; he came the fifth time heading for Hajj in Sha'ban of the year 355 AH. He stayed in Mecca for seven years. He narrated in Mecca and Baghdad the Jami' of Muhammad ibn Isma'il (al-Bukhari) from al-Firabri, and it is the most noble of narrations due to the greatness of Abu... from the Sahih.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry on Abu Zayd al-Marwazi, a prominent Shafi'i scholar. It begins with a chain of transmission linking to Abu Bakr ibn Furak, who notes that Abu Zayd studied under Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, indicating his Ash'ari affiliation. The text then lists his exceptional qualities: he was the most retentive of the Shafi'i school, the best in legal reasoning, and the most ascetic. Details of his travels to Nishapur are given: he went multiple times for study, military expedition, and finally for Hajj in 355 AH. He stayed in Mecca for seven years and narrated Sahih al-Bukhari from al-Firabri in Mecca and Baghdad, with the note that this is the most noble transmission due to the greatness of Abu (likely al-Firabri). The entry emphasizes his scholarly stature and asceticism.

Key Terms
أبو زيد المروزي
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو بكر بن فورك
الجامع لمحمد بن اسماعيل
الفربرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'benefited from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari' means he was a direct student of Ash'ari.
Correction: The phrase 'ممن استفاد من أبي الحسن الأشعري' does not necessarily imply direct study; he may have benefited through works or intermediaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The chain of transmission for Sahih al-Bukhari via al-Firabri is called 'the most noble of narrations'. How much do you value the chains of knowledge that connect you to the Prophet? What are you doing to safeguard that connection in your own learning?

Poem Praising Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki
Panegyric poem extolling Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki's virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reverential tone and theological significance of this panegyric poem honoring Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki.

Core Idea
A poem captures how one scholar's knowledge revived the Sharia.

This poem by an anonymous admirer celebrates the towering scholarly stature of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. The poet uses vivid metaphors of a surging sea and pearls to convey the unmatched depth of Abu Sahl's understanding. Through this lesson, you will explore how such panegyric verses serve as historical testimony to the reverence scholars commanded in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once, then its English translation. Let the poetic rhythm and imagery resonate before analyzing the content.
The poem uses classical Arabic poetic devices; don't worry about every word at first.
2
Identify the main claims about Abu Sahl's knowledge
Look for lines that specifically describe his impact: reviving the Sharia, being unrivaled, and his thought surging like a sea.
The poet explicitly states 'his knowledge revived the Sharia' and 'there is none like him'.
3
Examine the poetic imagery
Notice the similes and metaphors: sea, pearls, hoof reaching highlands. What do they convey about his stature?
The sea metaphor emphasizes depth and inexhaustibility.
4
Reflect on historical context
Consider why such poems were composed: to immortalize scholars, defend their legacy, or express gratitude.
This poem likely served as both tribute and testimony to Abu Sahl's authority.
5
Consider potential misinterpretation
The hyperbole might seem exaggerated, but in its historical context, it reflects genuine reverence. Distinguish poetic license from factual claim.
Scholars were often praised in lofty terms; the key is the core truth of his contribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحار أبا سهل وفيك تحيرى وما انا في مستعجم الأمر حائر
فيا عجبا من واحد سبق الورى فمافيهم مثل له ومفاخر
لعمرى لقد أحيا الشريعة علمه ولولاه اضحى رسمها وهو دائر
مساميه يبغى ابعد الشأو في العلا وهل مدرك شأو المها (قط حافر
ألا اقصروا أنى لكم مثل فهمه وذلك بحر موجه الدهر زاخر
هم يسهرون الليل في ضبط حجة تزول اذا ماجاش للشيخ خاطر
هو الصدر والمتبوع في كل مجلس وعن رأيه العالي مـبـاهـيـه صـادر
اغار عليه حين ينثر دره اذا وطيء المنشور من ذاك باقر
ويوحشني مهما يساميه مفحم كليل بطيء بالسفاهة خابر
ودادى له هز القريض وصاغه ومالي من طبع ومـا انـا شـاعـر
بلوت فما فيهم سواك مظاهر فأنت امام الدین عندى ظاهر
بقبت وسهلا ما اقام متالع وما ناح قـمـرى وغرد طائر
I am confused, Abu Sahl, and in you I am perplexed, and I am not in an ambiguous matter perplexed. O marvel of a single one who preceded all people, there is none like him among them and no glories. By my life, his knowledge revived the Sharia, and without him its trace would have been obliterated and it would be in decline. His rival seeks the farthest distance in highness, and can a hoof penetrate the distance of the highlands? No, desist! How can you have the like of his understanding? That is a sea whose waves are always overflowing. They stay awake at night mastering a proof that vanishes when the scholar's thought surges. He is the master and the followed in every gathering, and from his lofty opinion his emulators derive. He challenges when he scatters his pearls, when the spread is stepped on, that one is [؟]. He frightens me whenever he competes with a silent, dull one, slow, familiar with foolishness. My friend, he has shaken poetry and composed it, while I have no natural talent and am not a poet. I have tested and found among them no one like you. So you are outwardly the imam of religion in my sight. May you remain in [؟] and [؟] as long as [؟] stands and the dove coos and the bird sings.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a panegyric poem praising Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki. The poet expresses his amazement at Abu Sahl's unique excellence, stating that his knowledge revived the Sharia and that without him it would have been lost. He contrasts Abu Sahl's rivals as unable to reach his level. The poet describes Abu Sahl as the master in every gathering, and his thought surges like a sea. The poem ends with a supplication for his enduring presence.

Key Terms
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
الشريعة
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem's praise is mere flattery or exaggeration.
Correction: While poetic hyperbole is present, the praise reflects genuine high regard for the scholar's impact. Such poems often carry historical weight and indicate the respected status of the scholar within the community.

Checkpoint
What does the poet claim about the effect of Abu Sahl's knowledge on the Sharia?
A) It destroyed it
B) It revived it
C) It changed it
D) It ignored it

Key Takeaway
This poem illustrates how scholars like Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki were celebrated as revivers of religious knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poet claims Abu Sahl's knowledge revived the Sharia. What is one teaching of Islam that has become 'dead' or neglected in your own life? What specific action will you take this week to revive it?
?
You are not Abu Sahl—but you also have influence. Who in your circle looks to you as a source of guidance? Are you embodying the knowledge you have, or are you content with merely possessing it?

The Dream of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki: The Power of Good Opinion of Allah
Dream narrative confirming the spiritual state of Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual significance of having a good opinion of Allah (husn al-zann billah) as demonstrated in a dream narrative about the renowned scholar Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki.

Core Idea
Good opinion of Allah is the key to spiritual elevation.

This lesson presents a brief but powerful dream narrative from the biographical work of Imam Ibn Asakir. In it, a student sees his teacher, the revered scholar Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, in a dream in a state of sublime beauty. When asked how he attained such a state, the teacher gives a simple yet profound answer: it was his good opinion of Allah. This short story encapsulates a core spiritual principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of transmission (isnad) and the content of the vision.
The isnad shows the care in preserving such accounts.
2
Identify the key phrase
Pay attention to Abu Sahl's response 'بحسن ظني بربي' – what does it mean and why is it repeated?
The repetition emphasizes the centrality of this answer.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the state of the dreamer is described as 'beautiful, indescribable' and how this connects to the importance of husn al-zann.
4
Apply to your own spiritual life
Think about how cultivating a good opinion of Allah can transform one's state in this life and the next.
This virtue is accessible to every believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر بن الأستاذ أبى القسم قال انا أبي قال سمعت أبا بكر بن إشكاب يقول رأيت الأستاذ أبا سهل الصعلوكي في المنام على هيئة حسنة لاتوصف، فقلت له يا أستاذ بماذا نلت هذا؟ فقال بحسن ظنی بربی بحسن ظنی بربی.
Sheikh Abu al-Muzaffar ibn al-Ustadh Abi al-Qasim informed us, saying: My father informed us, saying: I heard Abu Bakr ibn Ishkab say: I saw Ustadh Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki in a dream in a beautiful, indescribable form. I said to him: O teacher, with what did you attain this? He said: By my good opinion of my Lord, by my good opinion of my Lord.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a dream narrative from Abu Bakr ibn Ishkab, who saw Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki in a dream in a beautiful state. When asked how he attained such a state, Abu Sahl replied that it was due to his good opinion of Allah. This illustrates the importance of having a good opinion of Allah (husn al-zann billah) as a means of attaining spiritual excellence.

Key Terms
أبو سهل الصعلوكي
حسن الظن بالله
الرؤيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dream indicates intercession or special favor due to Abu Sahl's status as a scholar.
Correction: Abu Sahl himself attributes his state to his good opinion of Allah, not to his knowledge or piety. The emphasis is on sincere trust in Allah, which is accessible to every believer.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Sahl attribute his good state in the dream to?
A) His knowledge
B) His asceticism
C) His good opinion of Allah
D) His lineage

Key Takeaway
Having a good opinion of Allah is a powerful spiritual practice that can lead to extraordinary states in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you die and someone sees you in a dream, what do you think your form would look like? What does that thought reveal about your current opinion of Allah?
?
You say you trust Allah, but when you face hardship, do you genuinely expect good from Him, or do you fall into despair and negative assumptions? How can you train your heart to truly have husn al-zann, starting today?
?
If your good opinion of Allah were the only thing that defined your life, would you be pleased with that legacy? What is one concrete action you can take this week to strengthen your trust in Allah's mercy?

The Stature and Travels of Abu Zayd al-Marwazi
Biographical entry highlighting Abu Zayd's status and travels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Zayd al-Marwazi is considered a leading Shafi'i scholar and the importance of his transmission of Sahih al-Bukhari.

Core Idea
Abu Zayd al-Marwazi combined deep knowledge of Shafi'i fiqh with asceticism and a key chain of Bukhari.

This lesson introduces you to Abu Zayd al-Marwazi, a towering figure from Khurasan who studied under the Ash'ari tradition and became renowned for his mastery of Shafi'i law and his ascetic lifestyle. The passage from Ibn Asakir's work highlights his virtues, his multiple journeys, and his prestigious narration of Sahih al-Bukhari, which is considered one of the most authoritative chains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the biographical details and the structure.
2
Identify Abu Zayd's three virtues
The text lists three qualities: best memorizer of Shafi'i school, best in reasoning, and most ascetic. Note how these set him apart.
These virtues are emphasized to show his scholarly and spiritual excellence.
3
Trace his travels
The passage mentions multiple trips to Naysabur, including a fifth visit for Hajj. Note the pattern of seeking knowledge, jihad, and pilgrimage.
His travels reflect his dedication to learning and worship.
4
Understand his narration from al-Firabri
He narrated Bukhari's Jami' from al-Firabri. This is considered a noble chain. Think about why chain matters in hadith transmission.
Al-Firabri is a key transmitter of Bukhari, making Abu Zayd's narration highly valued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو زيد المروزى رحمه الله ذكر أبو بكر بن فورك انه ممن استفاد من أبى الحسن الأشعرى من أهل خراسان قرأت على أبى القسم زاهر بن طاهر المعدل عن أبي بكر أحمد بن الحسين الحافظ قال انا أبو عبد الله محمد بن عبد الله الحافظ قال : محمد بن أحمد بن عبد الله الفقيه الزاهد أبو زيد المروزي ، وكان احد أئمة المسلمين احفظ الناس لمذهب الشافعي وأحسنهم نظرا وأزهدهم في الدنيا قدم نیسابور غير مرة أولها للتفقه قبل الخروج إلى العراق وبعده المتوجهه إلى غزو الروم وقدمها الكرة الخامسة متوجها إلى الحج في شعبان سنة خمس وخمسين وثلاثمائة وأقام بمكة سبع سنين وحدث بمكة وببغداد بالجامع لمحمد بن اسماعيل عن الفربرى، وهى اجل الروايات لجلالة أبي زيد
And among them is Abu Zayd al-Marwazi, may Allah have mercy on him. Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned that he was one of those who benefited from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari from the people of Khurasan. I read upon Abu al-Qasim Zahir ibn Tahir al-Mu'addil from Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Hafiz, who said: Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Abdallah al-Hafiz informed us, he said: Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Abdallah, the faqih, the ascetic, Abu Zayd al-Marwazi. He was one of the imams of the Muslims, the most memorizer of the Shafi'i school, the best in reasoning, and the most ascetic in this world. He came to Naysabur several times: the first time for fiqh study before leaving for Iraq, and after that heading to the campaign against the Byzantines. He came the fifth time heading for Hajj in Sha'ban of the year 355 AH. He stayed in Mecca for seven years. He narrated in Mecca and Baghdad the Jami' of Muhammad ibn Isma'il (al-Bukhari) from al-Firabri, and these are the noblest narrations due to the greatness of Abu Zayd.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage introduces Abu Zayd al-Marwazi as a prominent scholar from Khurasan who studied under Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (through his chain). It highlights his three virtues: he was the best memorizer of Shafi'i fiqh, the best in legal reasoning, and the most ascetic. It details his multiple visits to Naysabur: first for study, then for military campaign (probably jihad against Byzantines), and fifth for Hajj at age around? It mentions his seven-year stay in Mecca and his narration of Sahih al-Bukhari from al-Firabri, which is considered the most authoritative transmission.

Key Terms
أبو زيد المروزي
أبو الحسن الأشعري
مذهب الشافعي
صحيح البخاري
الفربرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'أحفظ الناس لمذهب الشافعي' means he was the best jurist overall.
Correction: It specifically means he had the most memorization of Shafi'i school, not necessarily that he was the best jurist in all respects.
